## The story of DBT-C: In conversation with Dr. Dunkley (British Isles DBT clinician/trainer/author)

Well, hello. My name is Dr. Francesca Pereplyotchik. And today I have a pleasure of being interviewed. As you all know, those of you who visit my channel regularly, I am the one usually interviewing somebody. All those amazing researchers and um who come to talk to us about their adaptations of DBTC and about the experiences. And today I thought it would be a great opportunity to finish up the year because it's going to be 2 years of my YouTube channel. Right? That to um finish it up with getting to interviewed because I think that it would be interesting at least for some people to understand how all of this was developed, why did I get to where I am right now, and what started all of this. This is just to to for people to know that, you know, there's just so much wealth of the material out there, and Christine is the leader in the world of DBT. So, please dive into her argument. Anyway, Christine, thank you so much for being here. And I just want to say that the idea of the interview did not come from me. It came from Christine and some of the trainings that we're organizing. And we decided, "Hey, why don't we just use it also for my YouTube channel." All right. Well, I did not want to miss an opportunity to just first of all, find out a little bit more about you because you are such a character, Francesca. You are such a character. And also to to find out how you got into um working with children, and then some of your prized insights, the things that you really want everybody to know about. Um and you will be coming over to British Isles. Well, actually you're going to be doing online, aren't you? But you're doing a training for us in our time zone so that um we can find out all about DBTC. So, this is why when you said, "Come on," I was like, "I want to interview you. I want [laughter] to find out about you." And let's see. So, tell me. Start by saying, you know, when you do psychology, there's so many choices of where you could work. How did you get attracted to working with children and their parents? Um well, it's always an interesting question about the path. How do people end up where they are, right? And one of the things that we talk in DBT for children is that we have this understanding of um and since we work with children, um I use all kind of metaphors. And metaphors are embedded in DBT to begin with, right? The metaphor that I'm using because they understand computers, I'm using the the um the computer as a way to explain to them how things work. And I say, "Our brain is a biological computer for all intensive purposes it is, right?" You know, the way it functions with our frontal lobe being the main processor, with, you know, how it gets the information in, having a memory banks and all this. Now, as any computer, there is hardware and there is software. Hardware is what we come into this world with. Right? Our instincts, of course, our reflexes, but most importantly, our talents, our learning differences, our interests, proclivities, right? You know, things that are already pre-programmed, even predispositions to physiological disorders, right? Some people have more problems with losing hair, some people with thyroid issues, some people with joints, whatever it may be, right? Predisposition to psychological disorders, right? Some people are predisposed biologically to be reacting to stress more with fear. Therefore, they may develop much more prone to develop anxieties. Some people may be reacting to stressors with sadness, right? They might predisposed to depression. Some people with anger and so forth, right? All of that is installed. And as soon as that hardware starts to transact with external environment, it starts to develop software. That's the learning that we sustain during our lifetimes, all the programs that we build. Anytime we think something, anytime we make an interpretation, anytime we make meaning, anytime we do something, that's a program written in our brain of what to do next time in a similar situation. Right? So, coming back to your question, how did I end up being here where I am? Well, [clears throat] some people have pre-programmed talents that are very easy to see what that is. My daughter, for example, right? Since she was three, she was already trying to draw. She was just barely using, you know, her fingers, no fine motor skills, but she was And And indeed she became an artist. So, it was very easy to see. For me, [clears throat] I had so many interests. Number one, when I was And I come from um former Soviet Union. I lived in Republic [clears throat] of Ukraine that is now a country. But so, I do come from former Soviet Union, which also has um one of the factors why I decided to be a child psychologist, but we'll get to this later. But um back then, I had a luxury and a privilege to actually um be an actress. So, I was a child actress. I have like I I I actually don't remember exactly, either 13 or 14 movies, episodic roles, some maybe supporting actress, but it it was fabulous. It was such an experience. Oh my god, I got to miss school, by the way. And I would go to all of those locations and be with all those famous actors and do all kind of fun things. And but what it helped me to do was develop this comfort being in front of the camera. Right? This comfort in presenting myself and um I'm not being afraid of audiences because as you can imagine, as camera is on you, there are 200 other people, there are like lights and this and makeup, right? And everybody's looking at you. And all of that, of course, is a huge asset for me to do what I do right now, present [clears throat] in front of the audiences, having YouTube channel, being very comfortable with myself in front of the big crowd. Now, the other thing, right? Is I wanted to be a surgeon. A brain surgeon, right? Uh but I The thing is that I recognized early on one thing that I had from early on somehow built in, I was very honest with myself. And I knew I am [clears throat] not Meryl Streep. I'm not going to be a great actress. Okay? I was like, "All right, that's fun, but that's not mine." Um wanting to be a surgeon, fantastic and wonderful. I don't get physics. I don't get math. I don't get chemistry. So, I would probably just not graduate. I also wanted to be a sculptor. Um yes, [clears throat] I you know, I I used to make, you know, wonderful things out of the clay. And I continued to use that like for my kids, like making Halloween costumes for them and stuff like that. But I was not accepted into the Academy of Arts back in Ukraine because I cannot draw. And you see, it's interesting how we proceed from one thing to another. It's almost like we have multiple doors open, but somehow doors are closed for us, right? And sometimes we close them ourselves, like with acting. And we still continue to proceed in a particular way, but still use all of those aspects that we learned, the programming, right? That end up the cumulative effect with a qualitative jump of finding your niche, what fits you best. And I think that what I do, being a child clinical psychologist, is indeed fitting me best in what I love to do because I am so passionate about I don't work. Yes, I am on in sessions with my clients pretty much 12 hours a day. One client comes in, leaves, another one. Pretty much like that. On weekends I do trainings with clinicians, right? And I try to write books and do research all in between. So, I work a lot, like 12, 13 hours a day. But I don't get tired because I'm passionate about it. Right? So, I get to do what I love to do. And working with clients is so amazing and wonderful um because most of I get to cry with them. &gt;&gt; [clears throat] &gt;&gt; I get to laugh with them. And my my son actually um he's 19, uh Danny. He also decided to be a psychologist. And what Of course, he's pretty you know, pretty pretty predetermined, I guess, too, because he's a sensor. He's very emotionally sensitive person. He can read other people. Other people really come to him for advice. And also, he took all of my trainings, of course, you know. I've been using all of this on him. And I mean, he came to me and he said, "Mom, you're sitting home." Yes, after pandemic, I mostly work from home. "You're laughing with your clients all day long. You get money for it. I want to do the same thing." Uh and he's also a Capricorn. He said, "Mom, um he knows what he wants. "You got to teach me everything. You're going to give me all of your practices, and then you're going to retire." Well, like, "Okay, no problem." &gt;&gt; [laughter] &gt;&gt; So, um &gt;&gt; [clears throat] &gt;&gt; the progression on how things build on each other is incredible, right? And for me, I found that niche in which I can be passionate about what I do and help people. I'm actually doing something that I can see the changes. I can see how those children that come to me like depressed, anxious, temper tantrums, their poor parents are burnt out, overwhelmed. They don't know what to do. They're so stressed themselves. Because if you think about this transaction between the child who comes in emotionally sensitive and with more needs in how to address that emotional sensitivity, impacts it on the external environment that just doesn't know what to do with it because we don't come with instructions. And emotionally sensitive children, good enough parenting is just simply not good enough. They need super parents. We don't come with knowledge of how to be super parents. So, this child is impacting the environment, dysregulating the environment. And the more dysregulated the environment impacts on the child dysregulating the child. So, emotional sensitivity by itself does not mean emotional dysregulation. It's a process. It's a product of this transaction, maladaptive transaction. And through that transaction, we have two products. &gt;&gt; [clears throat] &gt;&gt; Emotional dysregulation, behavioral dysregulation, and invalidating environment. Most of the time, it doesn't stop that way. Most of the parents that I see, they're wonderful, caring, good enough parents. They may have other children. They're fine. And this one is climbing walls. What's going on? Because good enough parenting is just not good enough, and which dysregulates the environment. So, I see those families coming to me like everybody's stressed. And through a lot of work and determination, they can get to the point where everybody can indeed transact in an adaptive way. And that's that's, you know, that that's the biggest reward that I can get out of this. So, how did I become a clinical psychologist? All of that was building on top of each other. So, I was able to get learning from multiple sources. And as I said, former Soviet Union, as you can imagine, [clears throat] in former Soviet Union there was no such thing as validation. There is not even a word validation in Russian language. It just doesn't exist. We needed to translate it, and now it's validatsiya. It's just validation with Russian accent, pretty much, right? It tells you a lot. If the language does not have a term for a concept, the concept doesn't exist, right? And most of that was based on punishment, screaming, yelling, criticism, judgment, blaming, shaming, downward comparisons. And our parents were not doing that because they didn't like us or didn't love us or were trying to do something that wasn't good to us. No, with best intentions in mind. They were also parented this way. Parenting is the model, is the program that you're building in your child's mind of how your child is going to parent their children, right? So, they just didn't know better. And I did not like it much, right? And from even early childhood, I never held it against anybody like in my family when they were doing it because I knew that they were doing it with best intentions in mind, and I was not a very good kid. I [clears throat] was very stubborn. I continue to be. My way or highway. However, I'm not rigid. You can definitely change my mind if you give me enough of the argument. I will absolutely listen, and I will change the way I think. But unless you do that and unless you're convincing enough, you can kill me. I ain't going to budge, you know. So, being a child in former Soviet Union with this type of a characteristics born with was not very conducive to mellow childhood. So, as a kid, I was like, "This probably there are other ways. There must be other ways." I understand why, but there must be other ways. And that kind of like stuck in me. And as I I was progressing and um um uh becoming like an adolescent and uh luckily, in other very important piece to that, I decided to become a teacher of English language and literature because I loved children. I always loved children. So, I like I wanted to be with children. There was no such thing as the field of psychology in there, only psychiatry, right? So, I could not become a psychologist, but I wanted to work with children. And uh because of that, I came to America when I immigrated right before former Soviet Union collapsed in 1991, I came equipped with a language. Right? So, that was another huge piece. And then when I came here, it was like, "All right, I'm going into psychology now." And that's how I started to get into the field into the passion that I actually ended up doing. And another huge piece to that was my mentors. So, I had a privilege, a luck, and immense opportunity to have Alan Kazdin, uh who is, you know, Einstein of the field of child psychology. You know, before him, there was no such field in in in in America, at least. But so, he introduced child psychology to the field, and he's a behaviorist. So, I had a big huge behavioral background because of that. So, he was my mentor when I was in Yale receiving my PhD. So, all of those were steps. And in Yale, when I was receiving my PhD, there was another person who became my mentor after when I was doing my internship. Uh Seth Axelrod was, you know, I had a privilege of Seth also being my mentor when I was an intern. And we continued to collaborate for many, many years afterwards until unfortunately, he passed away. But he was the one who came to left give a lecture at Yale for one of the seminars, and he talked about DBT. And I was like, "Wait a second. Dialectics. I know that word. You know how I know that word? That's the only thing that I retained from learning the writings of Marx and Engels that I had to &gt;&gt; [laughter] &gt;&gt; Back in Soviet Union, you had to read Lenin and Engels, right? And Engels, taking from that from Hegel, right? He introduced the notion of the dialectics and the three principles of dialectics. And I remember that. That was probably the only thing I remembered from him. I was like, "Yeah, that actually makes sense for me even my background." And that juxtaposition, seeing both sides, I was like, "Oh my god. That totally fits. What are they doing with that? They're doing it for adults. Oh, wow. And they can treat this and that with this. And they're doing it for adolescents. Why nobody is doing it for children? Hmm. Why can't I do it for children?" And that's how DBTC was born. And this is so forward-thinking because everyone would say, "Oh, no, you can't do it with the children, you know, they're too small." And even with adolescents, "Oh, you can't do it until they're over 13." But you were just ready to do it. Absolutely. Absolutely. Now, as I learned and developed and adapted Marsha Linehan's model, and of course, taking into account DBTA, right? And Rathus and Miller, you know, approaches and thinking about it, um right now, what it is, it says DBTC for children. But DBTC for children is not for children. DBTC for children is a model. Because it's parents first approach. It's not about the child. And when I say the child, I don't mean an 8-year-old. I mean you're 80 and your child is 60. I mean you are just considering starting a family. And any child can benefit from super parenting, right? We need to get a license to fish. But for some reason, we don't need to be educated on how to parent. Everybody think that they're going to be a wonderful parent. And when I ask them like, "Where is it coming from? How were you parented?" Like 99% of the time, I get, "Well, my parents made some mistakes. Well, actually, a lot of mistakes." Well, like, "All right. So, where are you going to get the knowledge to do something different?" And they say, "Well, I will do everything opposite to what my parents did." To which I say Yes, exactly. Number one, exactly. But the thing is that it's not humanly possible because your parents wrote programs in your mind on what to do. A child who's being physically punished by a parent, right? Is not laying down, right? Crying and thinking, "Oh, what a wonderful parenting technique to teach me discipline, right?" He's probably thinking, "I will never ever do this to my children." And then what happens when they have their own children, they do the same. This is the foundation for transgenerational transmission of violence, right? We teach our children on our our model on how to parent their children. So, I say, "Okay, let's say that you're superhuman. And you indeed can do do everything opposite to how your parents parented you. You know what not to do. Do you know what to do instead? Do you have a capacity to do it, right? So, I think that it's very very important to teach parenting in high school. My biggest, like, where I actually want to be when I finally retire, probably at the age of 120. I cannot imagine myself retiring. When I'm pretty much just rolled out out of session and I can no longer do any you know any any of that what I where I actually want to be what I actually want to achieve is this. I want to start when they're little. Elementary school. Teaching them how to give to myself. How to self-reinforce. How to self-validate. How to self-control. How to self-love. All the things that we call in DBTC for children, bake your own bread. Give, you know, attend to your own needs instead of relying on other people. Love me, reinforce me, validate me. Me, me, me, me, me, right? Take, take, take, take. No. It's important for other people to give to us. But if that's the only thing that we're relying upon and our needs can only be satisfied by other people giving to us and that's how we program. Who do you know that taught their child how to self-reinforce, audience? Do you know anybody? Simplest simplest ingredient of bread baking, self-reinforce. Just say, "Great job, me." Right? I'm not even talking about the caveat of that is not just great job, me. Great job, me on what you did. Not what you got. Outcomes don't belong to us, right? We can only be happy or unhappy about outcomes. We what belongs to us how what we contributed. You may have a negative outcome and still say, "Great job, me. I faced the problem. I did not run away. I did my best. I learned from it. Great job, me." And then self-validate on not getting what you want. "Oh, of course I'm upset because right?" So, we don't teach our children, but we we teach our children how to be self-advocating, self-critical, self-punishing, and rely on other people to validate, reinforce, support, whatever. And when other people don't give it to us, don't satisfy our needs, and that's the only source that we know to satisfy our needs, what's going to happen? We're going to take it by force. We're going to scream, yell, threaten, throw a tantrum, you know, suicidal ideation, self-harm as sometimes means to threaten other people to give us ex-squeeze it, right? So, we need to give to ourselves first. We need to bake our own bread. And then when other people give us just like fantastic. But when they don't, we have our own bread. We're not hungry. We are fine, right? And only then we can give to others. Because the foundation of healthy belonging, maintaining healthy relationships is about what? Giving to other people. But first you need to have something to give. If everything is like ripped out from others, have nothing to give. That's why teaching children just social skills is not going to do much. Because they don't have a capacity to use them consistently. They first need to give to others, right? But to give to others, they need to give to themselves. So, elementary school. How to bake your own bread. Giving to yourself. I just love it. I absolutely love it. I just love it. I'm so glad. &gt;&gt; It's so intuitive. It's so intuitive. I know. We know all of this. Christine, I'm going to you know go offshoot. We know all of this. We know all of this. We'll talk about other things such as core problem analysis, I guess, because it's part of of DBTC. CPA can be applied transdiagnostic because CPA core problem analysis is about identification of functions of the behavior. That's all. ## Demonstrating Core Problem Analysis (CPA) with my son Danny

**Duration**: 40:20 | **URL**: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Kno5ED-C76o

Hello, my name is Dr. Franchesca Perplug and today I have a pleasure of doing this YouTube video with my son Daniel. Hi Daniel. Thank you so much for being &gt;&gt; so [laughter] &gt;&gt; why I invited Daniel is because I would like to demonstrate actual core problem analysis with somebody who will be willing to do it. Of course, I cannot ask a client to do this and I don't want to do it with another fellow therapist because they know all of this and even though Danny also knows about CPA, he been CPA by me so many times in his life. So he does know the process. He did take my courses. So he is quite knowledgeable. So what you're going to see is a CPA with a pretend client that is actually knowledgeable of the entire process. And that usually what happens when you do several CPAs and at some point clients do catch up. All right. So Danny, what are we going to do CPA on today? Um I think my dissatisfaction with my hair. &gt;&gt; Oh, that's mutual. &gt;&gt; Okay. All right. So, um just for the viewers, if you haven't seen the videos about CPA and the core problems, please go back and look at them so that you'll have a better understanding what we are doing here with Danny today. And uh just to highlight repetition, CPA starts with catching the name. We call those maladaptive malware programs that are unconscious that we're trying to bring the level of awareness so that we can have a freedom of choice from them and override them with adaptive programming. We call them fish, right? I work with children. I don't want to say let's talk about your maladaptive program, right? We say fish by the analogy of our psyche having being like this um iceberg right the tip of the iceberg is our conscious awareness and below the waterline that's where our software lives that's where our hardware lives this is where all of the programs are actually stored and as I said gazillion times and we'll say one more time 90% of our reaction come from unconscious processes all of those programs s in our software and our hardware actually running our responses without us having any knowledge that that actually exists. And if they run if they govern our behavior without us even knowing their name or that they exist, of course they're going to have all the power, right? We don't even know what it is why we do what we do. That's why we continue to do the same thing expecting different results. And um Albert Einstein said that this is a definition of insanity. Well, Albert Einstein was a brilliant physicist. However, he did not know much about psychology. No, this is not a definition of insanity. There are reasons, there are functions behind it. All of this is caused there. Therefore, it's not a definition of insanity to do the same thing expecting different results. What is the definition of insanity? Is treating what is not real as if it is. A clinical example of that would be somebody having a psychotic episode and they're thinking that they're talking to somebody or seeing somebody that actually not there. Non-clinical example of making something that's not real real in our mind is my boyfriend broke up with me. He hates me. Therefore, I am worthless. Now everything that comes in our mental space can be real, maybe real, not real. What is real? My boyfriend broke up with me. What's maybe real? He hates me. Right? This is about another human being and therefore this is just a hypothesis. Maybe he loves you and his mother hates you and broke you up, right? And he'll love you for the rest of their life. What is never ever real is what we do all the time. something happened and therefore I doesn't matter what follows good bad worthy worthless right that is never real and this is the malware programs that we need to catch and bring level awareness so we have a freedom of choice from them to override them otherwise we'll just continue to be miserable and continue to code and recode that program acting on it as if it's real right? Making it real in our mind, making it stronger with coding, recoding and also not writing new programs in our mind that actually are adaptive and helpful. So this is what CPA does. It brings into the level of consciousness what are malware virus programs. Well, in what I do with children and adults, we just call them fish. Now, to get a fish out, to get that maladaptive virus program up so we can have a freedom of choice and overwrite it, we need to first have a hook. What's a hook? A name. So, especially for those uh audience members who have not seen the previous videos and the video that we had on CPA you know, by itself, please go back and and watch. But here is a very um a tiny intro and the gist of what CPA is all about and I will talk about how it developed. So, CPA is a core problem analysis. It is a model of assessment and intervention where what we are doing is we're taking the programs that are in our software that we're not aware of and 90% of our responses are governed by unconscious processes and if we don't know what the program is, we just keep doing what the program wants and even if it backfires, we continue to do it because there is a program and therefore there is a function. So, what we're after is understanding, kind of like dragging it from unconscious to the level of consciousness so we can have this moment of mindfulness and say, "Oh, hi my program that I am ugly, that I am useless, that I am worthless, that nobody loves me, that God knows what, right?" And our freedom of choice from it and override. So, in in a way, CPA, although may be quite irreverent in the process because during the process of CPA, we ask a question why. Why is it a problem? What does it do for you? What does it mean, right? And we don't like questions why because we are programmed that it implies criticism. When you ask so, "Why are you doing it?" meaning that the the person is criticizing you. So, this is automatic for us. That's why we don't like it. Plus, we're going into the what could be unallowable material, things that people don't want to touch and think about. So, it may not be something that is pleasant in the process. However, it's very validating in the outcome because you get this aha moment, "Oh, that's why I am doing this." And as we all know, validation is about understanding. So, it provides a very deep level of understanding of why we're doing what we're doing a function of the behavior, right? And once we understand what the function is, then we can get a freedom of choice from it and override how we uh respond to address, to meet that function. Instead of dysfunctional way of meeting the function, we can figure out adaptive way of either meeting the function or work on that function no longer being necessary for us. So, CPA has is a is is a what we can also called in DBT is a Socratic questions method. Uh the other way of looking at it, it's uh the technique that is based on CBT downward arrow, when you're going down, down, down, down to the core beliefs, right? Instead of core going to the core beliefs in DBT um C and in CPA in particular, we're going to the core sense. And as we have talked about so far, we have those three core senses, self-love, sense safety and belonging that no matter where you start, you're bound to end into one, two, or all three of them. And the primary functions of all responses, be it adaptive or maladaptive, is to decrease vulnerability in these three core senses. It just maladaptive responses may decrease vulnerability short-term, increase long-term, like self-harm, right? You self-harm, decrease in emotional arousal, you get short-term outcome and you have a whole new cans of worms for the future. Adaptive responses do the flip. They may temporarily increase vulnerability in three core senses and decrease it long-term. Why? Because as we're meeting challenges face-to-face, any challenge introduces a moment of uncertainty. Our sense of safety does not like uncertainty, right? Our sense sense of self-love does not like uncertainty and our sense of belonging does not like uncertainty. We're calling it going through the door of pain, right? In the moment when we're facing a challenge, our sense of safety, "Oh my God, what if I'll make a mistake?" Our sense of love, "Oh my God, if I what if I will not be good enough?" And our sense of belonging, "Oh my God, what if everybody will think that I'm a slob and no longer want to be with me, right?" So, how CPA was developed is was pretty much through my practice. My clients teach me everything. It did not start as a top-down approach, meaning it starts with concepts and then we were trying to figure out how it may relate and help with the clinical practice. It was the other way around. It's bottom-up. As I was doing functional analysis and my brain works this way, I always need to know why and what the function of the client's behavior is before I can figure out how to match it with the intervention. So, for me that was a no-brainer to go to a client and keep asking, "So, why do you need it? And what does it do for you?" I just didn't know that it was CPA. It started to become that model over time. And as I started to ask those questions, I started to see that there is a pattern behind the answers. And as I mentioned, invariably, regardless of where we're started, "I am ugly, nobody loves me, God knows what, right?" The end result would be that that program, right? Was at the bottom about vulnerability in sense of self-love and or sense of safety and or sense of belonging. It depends on the person. Sometimes just one is affected, sometimes all three are affected. And from recognizing that pattern and I have not yet found a fourth sense, I became curious, wait a second. Like an an aha moment for me, is this what it is? Is indeed our adaptive and maladaptive responses are there to decrease vulnerabilities in our core senses? As I said, short-term decrease, long-term increase in vulnerabilities for maladaptive responses and the other way around for adaptive responses. And that's how CPA started to unfold and then in communication with my client and doing all kind of different trial and errors, we were also able together with my clients to figure out, all right, now that we know what the problems are and what the vulnerabilities are and what is driving our responses, now that we know that it's maybe a vulnerability in one or two or three senses, what do we do with that? How do we indeed we address it adaptively and pretty much CPA is fully empirical model and only after that empirical data for it, which is anecdotal and now it's also empirically supported by because it was part of DBT for children trials, right? You know, randomized clinical trials. So, now we have empirical support for it on a level of randomized trials. However, it started empirically and then we started to talk about it more conceptually. What is a concept of self-love? What is a concept of safety? What is a concept of belonging? And it's it's what one thing that I want to highlight. Sometimes when I talk about you know, it's unconscious, right? We don't know about what's what, right? Because it's not on level of awareness. Yet, as soon as I mention unconscious, people start to think that this is psychoanalysis. It's nothing to do with psychoanalysis. CPA is a few purely cognitive model because it's about interpretations, it's about meanings, and then the products of those interpretations are our feelings and our actions. And what we're there to do, change the way you think, you'll change the products. All right, sorry to cut in like that or or cut you off. And we have so much to talk about with sense of belonging. So, um I know, right? My self-diagnosed verbal diarrhea, I just don't know when to shut up. So, I have to like, okay, now. So, and now I'm going to ask you, what is the sense of belonging? Where did it originate from and why do humans have it? So, our brain is not wired for us to be happy. Our brain is wired for us to be safe. Therefore, our brain will always orient us automatically to negative things because they are unsafe. And it takes effort to recognize and acknowledge positive things, right? We call it negativity bias. So, everything is about safety in this human existence, meaning that our brain is pre-packaged, pre-wired, pre-programmed to survive. Nature doesn't care if we're happy, nature cares if we live long enough to procreate. So, sense of self-love serves safety as an instinct of self-preservation, for example, right? And sense of belonging also serves safety as a need to affiliate and congregate. Because look at me, I'm not a predator. I don't have fangs and claws. I don't have the musculature of a predator. I don't even have a tail. Drop me with all of my knowledge and, you know, the human abilities in the middle of Serengeti, I'm eaten by lions. But drop me in there with a group of people, much higher probability of survival. We are group animals. Now, remember we talked about the sense of safety, meaning that it's about control. We are all control freaks, to whichever degree, right? Some more, some less. But we have to have that need for control because that makes us at least feel safe or gives us an illusion of safety sometimes, right? When we're trying to control other people and control things that we're not in control of instead of controlling the only thing we can, ourselves. So, sense of safety, pre-programmed, need for control. If um nature were to leave it like that, we would kill each other off, right, in trying to control. So, even our hardware, our pre-programmed right, instincts and reflexes and all the other things that our, as we call it, hardware comes in with, right? Even that is dialectical. On one hand, we have sense of safety, need to predict, need to control. And then nature balanced it out with inborn benevolence. Our need to congregate, affiliate, form attachment, connections, to take care of the young, of the old, of the sick, to be there for other people. This is pre-programmed and this is about belonging. Now, sense of belonging is an ability to um appreciate that other people within your family, within your groups, actually want you to be there, actually want your company, actually value your company, right? ## The Power of Self Reinforcement - Learn to Bake Your Own Bread - Child DBT / DBT-C

[Music] hello my name is Dr Franchesca PR chob today we're going to continue on our very important discussion on how to learn to give to ourselves provide for our own needs instead of awaiting for others to love us reinforce us validators supporter us how to learn to bake your own bread and we're going to discuss a very important ingredient of that bread baking which is called self transmitted or not transmitted from generation to generation unfortunately not a lot of us have been taught self-reinforcement instead we taught to self- criticize to self judge actually this is stemming from something that is actually very natural to us as humans it's called a negativity bias we as humans are pre-programmed it comes in our Hardware so to speak we come pre-programmed it is instinctual for us to orient to negative events because they may be unsafe so our sense of safety automatically orients us to something that may be unsafe and therefore we notice acknowledge and remember more negative events than positive events that applies also to Parenting a child we as parents therefore have higher tendency to see a negative event that's happen happing with our kid comment on it acknowledge it to ourselves and our child than a positive event right how many of you have seen two children playing nicely together uh sharing toys having fun and parents coming in on purpose and saying oh my goodness great job playing so nicely together I'm going to bring you some milk and cookies may happen sometimes however not not very often and not purposeful to reinforce that behavior instead is nice and quiet and parents like okay have this quiet moment to go and relax somewhere and as soon as squabble between the two kids start what do we do we rush we pay attention we criticize and we punish whatever the negative behavior that we see that is much more frequent negativity bias is one of the issues also you know our mood dictates us rushing and trying to get control over the situation get the control back now on top of that not only we are noticing more of the negative events for ourselves and for our children and comment on those we also treat positive behaviors a lot of the time as shs such as my child should be polite my child should um U follow my directions right away what are we doing here is we're treating behaviors that are learned they are learned responses we're not born with instinctual need to be polite and follow directions of our parents right away right we all want control right so we want to do the opposite of that instinctually now so we are not born with that it's not a reflex however we as parents somehow assume that my child should be polite should follow directions right away so we're treating learned responses as if they are reflexes as if they are instincts and therefore we're not providing the reinforcement that needs to be provided to the Le Behavior to increase probability of those behaviors happening and to maintain that behavior in future now so what is happening we are orienting to the negative we are automatically seeing it and acknowledging it while orientation to the positive events is requiring F it's not automatic we treat positive behaviors as if they should be happening like reflexes and therefore not commenting acknowledging and reinforcing them so the end result of that is what most of what we hear is children is feedback that is corrective feedback at best negative feedback on the negative behaviors and a lot of the time it's not only on what we did also coupled with who we are saying not only what you did was wrong but there is also something wrong with you for doing it and what you did is an empirical evidence to demonstrate that there is something wrong with you and all of that over time developed the this record it is playing in the back of our minds this I'm bad I'm defective I'm deficient what's wrong with me of course how else can it be that all of us are plagued by this record of self judgment and self-criticism and when a challenge comes my way and most of I remember is my failures because of that record because of my negativity bias because I was pre-programmed right for looking at the negative plus all the programming that I received from my immediate family in the environment all I remember uh most of what I remember is negative my failures a challenge comes my way therefore I will have self-doubt I will be much more likely to freeze run away avoid because all I remember is me failing I'll be afraid to tackle that problem and therefore I will not be very likely to be effective in solving those challenges instead I'll have a tendency to avoid again criticize myself on top of it and also increasing probability of me doing what's called orientation to the external locus of control right making other people responsible uh blaming them for what's going on waiting for them to help me and resolve my problems for me and save me so to [Music] speak now not only we're not taught to self-reinforce such as simply saying great job me mentally petting or maybe physically really literally petting yourself on the shoulder on top of that when our you know accomplishments finally are recognized that does happen what are we praised on we are praised most often on the outcome not on a process therefore we are praised on what we got not what we did however outcomes don't belong to us we don't control outcomes we only control our contributions to the end result saying to a child great job getting an A on your task congratulations on that a what you're reinforcing here is an outcome but that a does not belong to the child so many variables need to happen just like that for that a to happen and only one of those variables is your child's on that task that they don't discuss with the classroom maybe the teacher doesn't like your child that much and is reducing the great artificially again so many variables need to happen just like that an ability to directly influence is me what I do what I feel what I think my biology that's it therefore when we're praising our children it's very important to remember to praise on the process what they have contributed what is actually theirs they don't own the outcomes and when we praise them on outcomes we are putting them in a position to have unrealistic expectations increasing chances of perfectionism and of course teaching them attachment to [Music] outcome that's moment in life presents us with a new challenge and therefore presents us with an opportunity to self reinforce and or self validate if we Face the challenged if we um did not run away from it if we did our best if we learned from the experience all of that is great job me regardless of whether or not we got the outcome that we wanted so there are a lot of times that we can self-reinforce and self validate at the same time we can reinforce ourselves on what we contributed and then self validate that if even though we did our best we still did not get what we wanted and we're disappointed so we can do self-reinforcement and self validation at the same time now what self-reinforcement does it helps us to reprogram the way we think it takes us away from self-criticism and self-judgment which is the opposite of self- reinforcement and self validation and self-criticism and self-judgment is about I did something wrong and there is something wrong with me it's always personal anytime we self judge and self criticize we are tapping and destroying so to speak our relationships with ourselves that is supposed to be I just am neither good nor bad my responses may be useful not useful helpful not helpful I may succeed or fail however this is the dialectic that we want to continue to maintain and balance I just am neither good nor bad and I can succeed fail people like me people don't I make a mistake I um achieve Mastery it's always an end self-criticism destroys the stability of my relationship with me as that to an entity neither good nor bad so when we're practicing self reinforcement we're going away from self-criticism and we are pay paying attention to the response itself nothing personal in it wow I did a great job doing this great job me with that excellent fantastic again regardless of the outcome and it's very important to remember that what we are doing is self reprogramming what we're learning within the series of the lectures that I'm giving what what I'm offering when I'm teaching my courses to parents to children to clinicians is how to reprogram yourself on a level of Consciousness and self- reinforcement is one of those tools so a very important thing to remember when you're self-reinforcing it is actually critical to say great job me for doing this because it's not sufficient to just feel proud or acknowledge feeling proud because feelings are products of thoughts feelings don't reprogram our brain don't reprogram our software thoughts do the reprogramming feelings are products of the programs so don't just feel proud say to yourself pray job me and this is one of the ingredients of our baking the bread learning how to give to ourselves so that we also can have something to give to other people and initiating and maintaining healthy relationships depends on our ability to to give what can I give to others if I don't have parents remember for you to start teaching your child how to self-reinforce you first always need to start with yourself as with anything else start learning to self-reinforce yourself and there already got some questions coming in in um the first question is is social anxiety typical a typical problem for a super sensor how can you help a super sensor to cope or deal with social anxiety so yes there is a very high cability with social anxiety for super sensors specifically so for super sensors who have emotional disregulation now not all super sensors will become um emotionally disregulated however emotional disregulation means clinical level of vulnerability in our Three core senses self-love safety and belonging so self-love is kind of like being good enough to love myself and we start to create all kind of criteria all kind of conditions to be able to achieve that good enough in order for us to love ourselves which is insane by definition because self L is unconditional and we're trying to satisfy conditions to achieve something that's unconditional creating more and more vulnerability in our sense of self-love sense of safety where does the sense of safety come from ability to control what can we control in that life to any degree we can control other people we can control life circumstances the only thing that we can possibly control to any degree and that's not even a control it's what we call ability to directly influence is me what I do what I feel what I think in my biology and when I cannot control the only thing that I possibly can me because as a super sensor things happen and doesn't have to be much for me to have this huge emotional tsunami as the reaction therefore or especially when I'm emotionally disregulated that means that the next moment I don't know what's going to happen am I going to face that challenge run away from that challenge freak out I don't know I can control myself and I can't predict the only thing that I can predict to any degree what I am going to do and of course our sense of safety goes out of the window plus vulnerability in the sense of belonging if I don't feel comfortable with my own with myself if I don't feel comfortable in my own skin if I don't give to myself what I need what kind of relationships can I give you know develop and maintain with other people because healthy relationship with other people stem from my ability to give but for that I first need to give to myself to be able to give to others what can I give to me self validation self-reinforcement self-control cognitive self restructuring selflove self-compassion self-care these are all the ingredient of what we call baking my own bread satisfying my own needs now with that foundation in mind let me answer your question super sensors specifically when they develop emotional disregulation they will indeed have a severe problem with their sense of safety because they cannot control the only [Music] hello everybody my name is Dr fres perova the topic of today's discussion is validation as the main tool of manipulation we already talked about that the main goal of therapy is to teach us ourselves our Behavior our responses what I do what I feel what I think my biology and through that indirectly influence other people one of the ways to do this is called validation now validation is simply communicating yourself and other people that I understand what you just did your response makes sense to me given its cause even though I don't agree with it validation is acceptance not approval and it is the main foundation for change how does that work well let's start with we all work from scripts our sense of safety necessitates our ability to control the best that we can and be able to predict whether or not we will continue to maintain control in the future this means that in our mind even though it's not fully on the conscious awareness it is happening automatically we constantly writing scripts for example I am deciding to go to a store to buy groceries so I'm going to write in my mind a sequence of events I will get in the car I will drive to a store and I am going to park there I'm going to buy this list of groceries I'm going to drive back home and put it all in the refrigerator right and we want for outside life to follow our scripts we want to continue to be able right in our predictions that's why we really don't like when we are interrupted in our plans and when other people are proving us to be wrong now when we are trying to get something out of another person we also have a script in our mind of how this conversation is going to go I will say this he will answer that that and I will reply in that manner and we also want the conversation to follow our script and the other person is trying to make me follow the conversation using the script and um as you can imagine the only thing you're going to get out of this is an argument not a desired outcome and as you're trying to convince the other person that you're the one with a correct information or a perspective the other person is going to do the same meaning that the only products you're going to get out of that is getting each other angry and defensive and this is indeed insane tactic to improve chances of getting what you want instead what you need to use is manipulation to go under the resistance and one of the tools of manipulation as we said is validation validation is as we said just communicating to the other person I get it I understand where you're coming from very important to remember you are not approving you are accepting and everything has validity every perspective has validity and that's what we're highlighting in validation remember this world is based on a principle of dialectics two things not being the same being complimentary meaning that they're diametrically opposite night Day Life death man woman Ying Ying good bad therefore everything is valid and invalid at the same time Paradox is the only Norm of our existence and we can use that to our advantage now let me show you how it works with children a child is coming to his mother to explain what happen or to defend himself because he just had a fight with his brother he yelled at him and he is having a script in his mind of what the mom is going to say I'm going to say this my mom is going to answer that way and I'm going to reply that way and he's prepared probably to fight because he's expecting mom's response being well you shouldn't have you know yelling at your brother is not the way to solve problems listen of course this is true yelling at anybody is not the way to solve problems however this is not a tactic that is going to increase chances of helping your child to change his perspective on what to do next instead mom needs to pull something completely out of the script that the child has and say wait a second oh my goodness I would be so angry too if somebody broke my favorite toy what just happened mom deviated from the script instead of reprimanding or punishing she said something completely opposite which is exactly what the child wanted to communicate to the mother but she said it instead of him what are the products of this number one mom indeed put the child in a position when he did not have to defend himself also by communicating understanding and non no judgment and no criticism she decreased child's emotional arousal in a moment we have a lot of studies demonstrating that validation and self- validation indeed decreases our emotional arousal also she deviated from the child script which put him in a position of being confused of wait a second what do I do now what do I say now now she opened up a opportunity to move in with her own script and make the child play her game by her own rules and what is that game now she has an opportunity to actually discuss with the child what happened and what to do instead and give the child a capacity to do it by practicing the discussed decision in a moment and reinforcing it with praise points and now this is what's called manipulation if you would like to learn more please
